===== PAGE 1 =====
On Being A Mentsh
A High Holiday Thought
The most religious statement about the nature of man is found neither in the Bible, Talmud, nor any of the classical Jewish
text. Nor does it come from the mind or pen of outstanding religious thinkers or writers. Indeed, its authorship is anonymous
and probably multiple. It was frequently on the lips of simple untutored men and women. It is part of that wonderful folklore
created in the Yiddish language by Jews of Eastern Europe.
We are talking about being a mentsh; the connotation given in that culture, now tragically destroyed, to the term “human
being”. A mother would express the hope that her son would grow up to be a mentsh. The individual who was slipping and
was spurred and admonished to try to make a mentsh of himself. The highest praise given a person was that he was truly a
mentsh.
One of the most significant convictions of religious beliefs is the high value it places upon man. Man is viewed as being
stamped with the Divine image, the object of God's love and concern. This doctrine is a correlary of religion's conception of
God, and the nature of the universe. It might even be said to possess an importance in the structure of beliefs not far below
that attributed to faith in God.
Hence the apology, or attempted rationalization as is contained is the oft-heard explanation - “After all, I am only human” - is
a denial of religion. Indeed, the single small word “only” is a masterpiece of both heresy and blasphemy. Being “human”
represents a goal and achievement. Being a mentsh means calling upon capacities which are sometimes permitted to lie
fallow. Man is never to be defined exclusively in terms of his present stature and attainments. There is more to him than what
he is at any given moment. Within him are possibilities unrevealed by his current posture; if he wills, he can move upward from
level to level. He never arrives-and this applies to the greatest geniuses-at the full realization of all his powers.
Man need not be a prisoner of his physical nature nor the victim of external pressures and circumstances. There is a
dimension of mentshlichkeit which is not encompassed by a physiological and sociological study of him. To realize this
dimension is man's greatest goal and adventure; to approach its fulfillment, his greatest accomplishment.
The High Holidays may have nothing more important to say to us than, in the time that we have left for us, to learn, to grow
to be a mentsh.
EAST COAST FRUIT CO.
Walter Portnoy - Mrs. Louis Portnoy and Families
The above thoughts were presented by the Portnoys with the hope that it will nurture some reflection on
our most precious possession - the gift of life.

Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge
The Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge was made possible
through the generosity of the Louis Portnoy family. This well-
equipped and well designed facility is used to serve the needs
of the youth of our congregation and the Jewish youth of the
entire community. It is the central location for formal
meetings, programs and activities, it also serves as a
synagogue for youth services.
Equipped with all sorts of games, such as pinball machines,
foozball, bumper pool, electronic and board games, the youth
lounge has something for everyone.

Henry Kramer Library & Resource Center
A book is the most delightful companion...An inanimate thing, yet it
talks...It stimulates your latent talents. There is in the world no friend
more faithful and attentive, no teacher more proficient... It will join you in
solitude, accompany you in exile, serve as a candle in the dark, and
entertain you in your loneliness. It will do you good, and ask no favor. In
return, it gives, and does not take.
The Henry Kramer Library and Resource Center continues to be one
of the most pleasant and inviting areas of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center.
As you enter the Henry Kramer Library and Resource Center there is
an attractive Reading Room. This serves as a warm and inviting place for
synagogue members to avail themselves of reading material. The Library
subscribes to many fine magazines such as “Moment”, “Commentary”,
“The Zionist”, “Congress Monthly”; and publications such as “Near East
Report”, “BA’Lam” “In the World”; and newspapers such as the
“Jerusalem Post”, “Jewish Post” and “Opinion”. The congregation is
invited to drop by and avail themselves of this material in this pleasant
surrounding.
The Solomon Schechter Day School Library has a very important and
required place in the education process for the Day School students and
the Hebrew School students. Mrs. Jill Derringer, Librarian, taught
library skills to the students which availed to them a great vista of books
for study and enjoyment.
The Library sponsored a “Sunday Morning in the Library”. Tobi
Meisel reviewed Paul Cowan’s “An Orphan in History”. Synagogue
members had an opportunity to view the many titles added to the Tofield
Collection through the generous contributions of the synagogue
members.
The Library Cart is available at the Kiddush every fourth Saturday of
the month. Center members have an additional opportunity to check out
reading books and periodicals.
Jack Becker and his committee have classified the Yiddish and
Hebrew books in the Library collection. They are available for the
congregation to use and enjoy.
Library committee volunteers continue to devote many service hours
necessary for the administration of this important facility. The Library is
grateful for the generous support of the Center membership. Member
contributions are used to purchase new books and library supplies.
Chairman: Minnie Schreiber
Tofield Collection

Book Burial
May 30, 1983
“K’vod Has-s’-fa-reem” “Honor for books” is extended to all books. As a mark of
special regard we have for GOD and a religious book, a Jewish book containing
GOD’s name--particularly a religious volume--is to be disposed of properly. If such a
book is beyond repair, defaced, or of no use to anyone, it is not to be put in the
garbage or burned. Rather it should be stored in the home or given to a synagogue
for safe-keeping or buried.
In a ceremony held only by Jews, a Book Burial took place at the Jacksonville
Jewish Center on Monday, May 30. Rabbi David Gaffney, Rabbi Dov Kentof and
Synagogue president, Sol Proctor and members of the congregation as well as
youth groups participated in the event.

Hebrew High School
The Hebrew High School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is the creation of the synagogue to offer Jewish youth an
opportunity to explore the broad expanse of resources available through our heritage. This program seeks to provide exciting,
innovative exploration of Judaism upon which each student might build. Students 8-11 grade are involved.
COURSE OF STUDY: Hebrew Language, Famous Jewish People, Jewish Philosophy, Holocaust, Comparative Religion,
Jewish History, Confirmation.
INSTRUCTORS: Dr. Neil Abramson, Carol Freedman, Rabbi David Gaffney, Shoshanna Haas, Rachel Hirsch, Rabbi Dov
Kentof, Elaine Pozin, Cathy Winterfield.
EIGHTH GRADE

Rose Lynn Plotkin and assisted by Marilyn Garber and Shirley Bielski.

teacher, Alina Kentof.

Passover
PASSOVER FOR ALL PROJECT
“Let all who are hungry come to eat” is recited during the Seder when the Matzah is uncovered. To insure that all will be
able to partake of Passover, the young people of each school at the Jewish Center undertook a most meaningful project. Each
student was requested to bring one dollar to school. These monies were used to purchase food stuffs for gift boxes to be given
to those families who, at this time, were unable to provide the foods of the holiday for themselves. Youngsters of our school
also called the congregation to contribute to this charity.
Members from each class were selected to purchase the food and the older students helped deliver.
Marlene Bossen shopped for Care Packages with Cheryl Bossen,
Katrina Echt and Tali Kentof.
Ellis Mass and Simeon
Abramson spent many hours
calling for “Moat Hittim”

Each year at this time we reflect on our past days and deeds and look ahead
to a healthy and prosperous year. A year of peace and freedom for all
mankind.

CHALUTZIM
General Aim: Jewish families today look to the synagogue as the center for Jewish activity: religious, education, social and
recreational. A major goal of the synagogue and its school is the education of youth with a firm base of knowledge, Jewish
identification of youth, a sense of pride in their heritage, and a commitment to Judaism. The goal of Chalutzim is to bring about
the internalization of Jewish values, attitudes and behavior patterns by supplementing the child’s formal religious education with
informal Jewish learning experience.

===== PAGE 30 =====
A Message From
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Youth and Education Director
Most of us seem to want continally to drag down the
image of God instead of coming up to His level. We lift
our eyes upward to the sky and think He is at the end of
the farthermost horizon, when actually that is only His
beginning. We think we have probed His secrets fully
when it comes to attendance at the University of the
Universe.
This brings to mind the marvelous story of the boy
who failed his entrance exams at Princeton University.
Before heading home he stopped off to see President
Robert Goheen and thanked him, saying, “I’ve learned a
lot here.”
President Goheen asked in amazement, “But you were never enrolled here. What could you
possibly have learned from us?”
The young man replied, “How little I know!”
Goheen answered, “Son, we will take you in. You are already two years ahead of the freshman
class.”
This is also true of the knowledge of Judaism that we have. You, the student of our
Supplemental school, have only begun to acquire your heritage.
Through the combined efforts of all of us, we will understand who we are and where we are
going. One human life is mystery beyond all comprehension. How much more must your
knowledge be of yourself and of God.
Alina, Gil, Tali and myself are extending our best wishes for a new year of happiness,
prosperity and peace.
Rabbi Kentof & wife, Alina
Dov s greatest thrill is hosting conventions.
Rabbi Dov Kentof.
Convention check-list
“Abraham” talks to children of the Sunday School.

Solomon Schechter Day School
P.T.A. President, Shirley Bielski The Selevan Computer Room
This past year has been an exciting experience with our dream of a Computer Center finally becoming a reality. The sale of
Magazine subscriptions, co- ordinated by Sharon Witten, enabled us to purchase eight Computer Units as well as educational
software.
We are so proud that our Selevan Computer Center will be available not only for our school, but for the Day Camp as
well as Adult Computer classes.
Our goal for the coming year is to add an Apple Computer which will enable us to incorporate Judaic software in our
teaching.
The P.T.A. is your organization and we encourage all parents to become actively involved in the school and its activities.
JEWISH CENTER RECEIVES FOUR AWARDS
AT THE VIENNIAL CONVENTION
The Biennial Convention of the Southeast Region of the United Synagogue of America was held the weekend of October
29 - November at the Eden Roc Hotel in Miami Beach.
The theme of the convention was “One Generation to Another Praise Your Work.” Scholar-In-Residence for the
weekend was Rabbi Benjamin Z. Kreitman, Executive Vice-President of the United Synagogue of New York.
One hundred thirty-five live-in delegates from six states were in attendance with Sunday’s delegates total increasing to
over 350.
Representing the Jacksonville Jewish Center were Rhoda Goldstein, Marsha Pollock, Rabbi Dov Kentof and Alina
Kentof. Center delegates were involved in all aspects . . . leading the ruach at the Oneg Shabbat the Birchat Hamazon,
session recording and presenting programs in several workshops. A creative Havdalah service was led by the Jacksonville
delegates and Stephanie Gray of the Center U.S.Y.
The highlight of the weekend came when the delegates accepted four Regional Awards on behalf of our congregation.
The categories awarded were: AFTERNOON SCHOOL and HIGH SCHOOL, WORLD OF OUR GRANDPARENTS
and the CENTER YEARBOOK and YOUTH ACTIVITIES. A plaque was presented and is hanging in the Center office.
A major address was presented on Sunday entitled. “The Vanishing Jewish Family.” The presentation explored the
characteristics of the current family trends and emphasized the synagogues role as a support system.
The social event was a “Western Night” which proved to be a great mixer. All in all the weekend was a comprehensive
and stimulating one. In order for our synagogue to survive the leadership must deal with current issues and trends so it can
meet the needs of all its member.
— Rhoda Goldstein and Marsha Pollock
Old Tyme goodness the entire family will enjoy!

Florida Branch
Spring Conference
Sisterhood officers and Board
enjoy dinner at the Center with
Fielen Applefield, Fla. Branch
President, Goldie Kweller,
National Consultant, Rabbi Dov
Kent of, Center President Sol
Proctor, Alina Kentof and
daughter, Tali.
Shalom-
At the midpoint of my term as Sisterhood
President, I look back at the many successful
programs and activities during the past year. Our
opening meeting was a rousing Family Feud
between the Schwartzmans and the Klotzmans.
The Annual Art Auction was financially
successful and a delightful evening for all who
attended. The proceeds from the Card Party allow us to purchase
items needed for the many functions of our synagogue and the Donor
Fashion Show helped us meet our ongoing financial committment for
the Sisterhood kitchen through the building fund. The sale of calendars,
silver polish, holiday flowers, new years cards, leagrams, personalized
items and our well-stocked gift shop provided additional financial
support for our programs during the year.
Throughout the year, Sisterhood provided assistance to the youth
and educational programs of the Center for Rosh Hashanah, Sukkot,
Simhat Torah, Hanukkah, Tu B'Shevat, Purim, and Passover
observances.
The highlight of the year was our synagogue's first women's minyan.
Our members conducted and participated in services for Rosh Hodesh
Adar, including the Torah reading and aliyot. Hopefully, this will be a
regular event in the future and allow our women the opportunity to
expand their participation in Jewish rituals.
During the year, Sisterhood adopted a family of Russian refuseniks.
We maintained a letter writing campaign to them so that they would
realize our awareness of their situation and that Jews around the world
must be concerned for each other.
The end of the year featured the Women's League Florida Branch
conference held here in Jacksonville. The conference allowed many of
our members to hear and meet the immediate past national president
Goldie Kweller, who attended as the national consultant. The closing
luncheon honored our Torah Fund contributors who helped us meet
our quota for support of the Seminary Residence Hall/Library.
As we move into the new year, Sisterhood needs your participation
and assistance to maintain the high standards we have set for ourselves
and I think you will personally benefit from your involvement. I hope the
spirit of warmth and enthusiasm which was present at our closing
luncheon will carry over throughout the new year.

Jewish Women
The National Council of Jewish Women is the nation’s oldest Jewish women’s volunteer organization. It was founded by
Hannah Greenbaum Solomon in 1893 and is currenly celebrating its 90th anniversary. Since its birth, NCJW has been
animated by a dedication to serving humanity and advancing the heritage of Judaism.
Today more than 100,000 members organized into over 200 Sections and Branches nationwide, continue a tradition of
community action and advocacy, focusing their attention on the priority areas of aging, children and youth, women’s issues,
Jewish life and Israel.
The Jacksonville Section of the NCJW is currently involved in projects that include an annual MENTAL HEALTH
FORUM, co-sponsored with the Mental Health Association and FJC, Pre-school vision screening, and the Art Enrichment
Program (“Picture Lady”) through which volunteers are trained by the Jacksonville Art Museum to introduce art to students
in both public and private schools. NCJW is also involved locally with providing programming for those “Seniors” that
participate in the Nutrition Program at Mt. Carmel, as well as co-sponsoring an annual SUPER PARENT SEMINAR.
With a grant allocated by the National office, the Jacksonville Section organized the CASA (Court Appointed Special
Advocate) program in our community. This program trains volunteers to act as a “friend of the court” in the best interest of an
abused or neglected child. This project is currently funded by the State of Florida and, in accordance with Florida l&w, is called
the Guardian Ad Litem program.
The annual statewide Tallahassee Institute provides our members with an opportunity to meet with our Legislators during
the Legislative session, to speak on behalf of our priorities, and to have our voices heard through an effective advocacy effort.
The major thrust of NCJW’s activities in Israel has been in EDUCATION AND SOCIAL WELFARE, in a number of
projects to aid in the development of Israel’s most precious resource, its children. The Research Institute for Innovation in
Education is one of NCJW’s programs in Israel; Ship-a-box, another.
NCJW seeks members within the Jewish community of all ages and interests.
An EVENING BRANCH has been organized to provide innovative and meaningful projects and programming for those
women who prefer meeting in the evening.
We invite you to join our celebration of 9 DECADES OF ACHIEVEMENT by becoming a member of the National Council
of Jewish Women.

efficiency-allowing equipment to run "full out" and putting

Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddisha
OFFICERS
President - Joel Bressler
V. Pres. - Nathan Sandler
Treasurer - Alan Taffet
Telephone Chairman - Ann & Nathan Shmunes
OTHER MEMBERS ARE
Linda Bressler, Bea Browdy, Laurie Benwick, Babs
Goldstein, Dr. Michael Greenburg, David Mizrahi,
Max Shmunes, Simon Selber, Anita Solomon, Lil
Meizlik, Lisa Lefkowitz and Bob Bossen.
STEPS WHICH SHOULD BE TAKEN TO FACILITATE SIMPLE JEWISH BURIAL ACCORDING TO THE CUSTOM
OF OUR COMMUNITY:
Upon a Jewish person's death an undertaker is called to move the body to the preparation room of the funeral home
where the features are set, bodily excretions are briefly washed off and the orifices are packed. The body is then wrapped in
a cloth sheet. A Hevrah Kaddisha committee (the Holy Society whose members have been trained to prepare a body for
burial) is called to the funeral home to wash the body which rests on a special wooden board, according to prescribed ritual.
While performing this work the committee recites certain prayers which befit the occasion. After the committee washes the
body, the members dress the body in white linen shrouds and place it in a plain, simple, wooden casket which is unadorned
except for a wooden Star of David which is attached to the top.
If the burial takes place on the same day that death has occurred, the body is transported to the cemetery for the funeral
services. If the funeral takes place on the following day (or later, if out-of-town relatives will attend), a “shomer” (watchman)
called by the Hevra Kaddisha sits with the body during the night; the body must never be left unattended. If it is desired to
have the funeral service in a chapel prior to interment, the chapel must be non-sectarian; that is, no Christian symbols may
be visible during the service.
The following persons must be called by the family or the undertaker as soon as possible after death has ocurred: the
rabbi, the Hevra Kaddisha, the person in charge of the Jewish cemetery, the burial vault company and the cemetery
association which contacts the grave-diggers.
In the event that death occurs in another locality, the out-of-town undertaker may arrange with either of two local funeral
homes, Hardage & Son-Estes Krauss or George Hewell & Sons, to use its facilities for the participation of the Hevra
Kaddisha and the shomer when applicable. These establishments will also indicate how to contact the above persons.
Finally, it is to be clearly understood that no embalming or cremation or public viewing of the body in an open casket is to
take place. If interment must be postponed beyond twenty-four hours, the body may be kept in a body cooler to retard
decomposition.

President's Message
As we approach another year, our thoughts review the many
activities of our Synagogue Center. The religious and Holiday services
continue to be meaningful and inspiring; the edcuational programs
have been interesting and innovative; the award winning Youth
Program continues to attract youth of all ages; and our recreation
program is unique and diversified.
Our Synagogue doors are always open.
Great emphasis has been placed on the Solomon Schechter Day
School and our Afternoon Hebrew School, for it is with our youth that
the future of Judaism rests. Our Day Care Program allows children of
working parents to spend a full day in a totally Jewish environment,
under expert supervision. With your support, we can insure that the
Jewish children of this community will always have a home away from
home.
Our school doors are always open.
As the “People of the Book”, we cannot allow education to be secondary. Our leadership has the knowledge, desire and ability
to make our educational program as outstanding as any private or public educational system in the country. To achieve this, we
must have your financial assistance - through donations to our various funds, trusts, testamentary bequests, donations of real
estate, and stocks and bonds.
The Tzedakah Box is always open.
The Baal Shem Tov said, “Mind is the foundation of Man. If the foundation is solid, the building is secure.” We must continue -
to do so, we ask you OPEN YOUR HEARTS and YOUR POCKETS.
Sol Proctor, President

===== PAGE 98 =====
Hadassah
Hadassah, the largest Women’s Zionist Organization in America, is a dynamic movement of 370,000, embracing action and
ideology, Spiritual commitment and practical achievement. The Hadassah organization had its beginning with a dreamer and
doer, Henrietta Szold, who in 1912, with two American trained nurses sailed for Palestine to establish a small welfare station
for maternity care and the treatment of trachoma in the old city of Jerusalem.
Today, the Hadassah motto, the words of the prophet Jeremiah--“Arachat Bat Ami”--“The healing of the daughter of my
people”, have seen the dreams come true when one visits the great medical center built on Mount Scopus and Ein Karem.
Hadassah has made an impact, through the years, on thousands of children, through Youth Aliyah, health an<Teducation,
rehabilitation and vocational training.
To work for the betterment of the people of Israel and the building of the nation of Israel is a basic tent of Hadassah
philosophy. An understanding of Hadassah is to understand two cultural heritages-Zionism and American democracy, from
which the Hadassah idea developed. It is work that gives its membership a deep sense of pride in belonging to an organization
which undertakes and achieves.
We have three groups, which are listed below. We welcome you to join us in our important work. For information call any of
the listed presidents or officers. This being the season of beginning, we wish you and all your family a very happy and healthy
New Year, L’SHONA TOV!

Kadima Convention-^
Three hundred Kadimaniks from 20 different chapters of the Southeast Region assembled for the largest Kadima
Convention ever. The theme for the weekend was “The New Anti-Semitism”. The Center’s Kadima Chapter conducted the
Shabbat Services and was responsible for many aspects of the weekend’s activites.
Steve Simenowitz, a Jewish blues singer, entertained Saturday evening. A carnival “topped off” the fabulous convention.
The following pictures illustrate some of the highlights of Kadima Convention '82.

Afternoon Hebrew School
~Y The Afternoon Hebrew School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is designed for the students in grades 3 - 2 and provides an element^
education leading to Bar/Bat Mitzvah and continuation in the high school program.
Teachers: Ted Gross, Rose Lynn Plotkin
Valerie Baruch, Jill Ghelerter, Carol Ann Hall,
Robert Hyken, Judith Jacobson, Melissa
Kirshenbaum, Laurie Paul, Jennie Schwab, Rachel
Thaler, Julie Snitzer.
Meeting twice during the week and on Saturdays, “the basics” are at the core of the curriculum, with stress on Hebrew Reading, Jewish
Social Studies, and current events relating to Israel are discussed thoroughly. Synagogue skills, holiday observances and knowledge of prayers
are all part of the comprehensive After Hebrew School program.
Eighth Grade Extension Class
Teacher: Alina Kentof. On Tuesday .afternoons and Sunday mornings, this group of eighth graders met to study Jewish history, Mishnah,
current events, and the Hebrew language. Class participants: Johnathan DePaul, Eileen Greenbaum, Kim Held, Jonathan Katz, Alex Kushnir,
David Lazarus, Manya Lazarus, Karen Manasse, Marc Selevan, Alyse Slaff.

She openeth her mouth with wisdom;
and in her tongue is the law of kindness.
She looketh well to the ways of her household,
and eateth not the bread of idleness.
Her children arise up, and call her blessed;
her husband also, and he praiseth her:
Many daughters have done virtuously,
but thou excellest them all.

Jewish Women's
Bowling
The Jewish Women's Bowling League
announces their new officers for the 1983-84
season:
President Jeanette Mayo
Vice President Doris Hochstadt
Treasurer Clarice Schiff
Handicapper Phyllis Winarsky
Sergeant At Arms Marilyn Freedman
Sunshine Lady Polly Copans
The League bowls on Tuesday mornings at 9:30
at Bowl-A-Rama on Phillips Highway. Anyone
interested should call Phyllis Portnoy at 733-1856.
B'nai B'rith
Youth Organization
The B'nai B'rith Youth Organization (BBYO) is
the largest high school Jewish youth organization
in the country. It is successful because it reaches
put to every and any Jewish youth, no matter
what sect of Judaism he follows.
The core of the program, Leadership Training,
provides knowledge and skills in a congenial social
atmosphere. Inherent in the organization is a
demand for commitment and involvement; the
fiber that holds Jewish youth together no matter
how small or large the community.
If you are interested and would like to get
involved in Jacksonville's AZA/BBG chapters just
call Robin Selber 733-6485 or David Burman 268-
0757.
Jewish Men's Bowling
The Jewish Men's Bowling League is the second
oldest league in Jacksonville.
Jewish men of all ages are invited to join for the
Fall '83 season. Bowling takes place at the Plaza
Lanes in Arlington Expressway on Monday
evenings 6:45-9:00.
For more information call Elliot Starr 724-2044.
Compliments
of
Laura Mae

President Mrs. Mitchell Peltz (Lois)

The River Garden Auxiliary Inc. formerly the Ladies Hebrew
Sheltering Aid Society is entering its 60th year of “Service With
A Heart”.
The Auxiliary continues in their tradition to assist and help the
River Garden Board to provide a Home for our Senior Citizens,
who are no longer able to live alone, a home where they can find
comfort, security, medical care and enjoyment, spiritual,
physical and emotional stimulation and pride in their own
accomplishments. All this combined gives the feeling and knowledge that “Someone Cares.”
The Auxiliary provides the extra budgetary needs of the Home, such as furnishings, redecorating, office and infirmary
equipment, and Hobby Shop materials. Funds are raised through our Annual Tombola, Donors, Social Printing, Fifth
Commandments Plaque, Dues, and Life Membership.
The residents enjoy the special parties planned and given by the Auxiliary for Chanukah, Purim, Mother’s and Father’s Day.
The Auxiliary sponsors the Red Cross Ladies in-house service and a Volunteer Program, and these ladies extend both their
hands and their hearts in service to our residents.
We welcome all of you to join us in this deeply human involvement.
We wish you and yours Peace, Health, and Happiness for a New Year.
Herman Paul

President Herman Paul

ENDOWMENT BOARD
Chairman Norman Hofheimer
The River Garden Home, situated on the banks of the St. Johns River surrounded
by beautiful gardens, is a vital integral part of the Jacksonville Jewish community.
There are few communities fortunate enough to have a geriatric care and
rehabilitation center that has the capacity and resources to provide 100% of the needs
of the local Jewish population. River Garden, therefore, is grateful for the involvement
and continued financial support of the community which enables it to offer all
residents a consistently high level of care regardless of their personal finances.
The staff of River Garden is called upon to deal with some of the most profoundly
difficult human care problems twenty-four hours a day, 365 days a year. Human
problems have no simple solutions, no perfect answers. River Garden is in the
forefront of developing decent human responses to these problems. In doing so, it has been singled out by the major government
and voluntary accrediting agencies as a model care facility.
Through individualized plans of care, the Home tries to strike a living balance between institutional requirements and
individual needs, in a residential setting supportive of Jewish identity. River Garden encourages residents to learn and develop
new interests and relationships by stressing the “wellness” of the individual. This positive accent on living, with a variety of social,
cultural and recreational programs helps create the special climate and tone of River Garden.
On behalf of the residents, staff, volunteers and the Board of Trustees of River Garden, we wish the entire community a New
Year of health and joy.
L ’SHONO TOVAH
River Garden Auxilliary

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morning and
evening in the Witten Chapel. Friday Family services, Sabbath and Holiday
Services are observed throughout the entire year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are observed
in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar
Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism,
Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery & Day Care with outstanding
program of secular and religious content. Hebrew school, carefully graded
with intensive Hebrew content, including Jewish history and customs,
Sunday School for kindergarten, first and second grades: Solomon Schechter
Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through 7th grades;
Hebrew High School with Pre-Jr. and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile and adult
Jewish books; Adult Education, religious and cultural Jewish courses; music
programs of religious and cultural Jewish music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood,
Ha’Rayim, a fellowship of young families; Center Couples Club, social
activities provided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation
Program (Olympic pool), summer week-days and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue Youth,
Kadima, Chalutzim, Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts, Brownies; Sunday
Funday; athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center Day Camp and
summer program at our 36 acre site.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . swimming, athletics,
snack bar; available for members during the summer months. Campsite
includes olympic-size swimming pool, 2 tennis courts, baseball, soccer and
football fields and playground.
For additional information, call the Center office — 268-6736

The Jacksonville Jewish Community Council was founded
in 1935 and became the Jacksonville Jewish Federation in
1979. We had many “homes” over the years but on June 5,
1983 a dream came true -- we reached a milestone in our
history when we dedicated our new home at 10829 Old St.
Augustine Road. Our Dedication Plaque which adorns our
lobby reads as follows:
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH
FEDERATON
1983 5743
This building. is dedicated to those in
this community who in the past,
present and future, with their acts of
kindness, love and giving, will not let
the Jewish people perish from this
earth.
We are looking forward to increasing our services to the
community. Our meeting rooms are available to the Jewish
organizations in our community and we invited everyone to
come visit.
We will continue to conduct the annual Combined Jewish
Appeal which raises monies for the United Jewish Appeal as
well as other national agencies. Our local beneficiaries are
River Garden, Jewish Family and Children Services and the
Community Kosher Nutrition Program.
We wish to announce to our community that on
September 1, 1983 Judah Segal will assume the
responsibilities of Executive Director of the Federation. We
know that under his direction we will continue to grow and
expand with the needs.

===== PAGE 137 =====
Summer Programs
There are many programs available for those students
who wish a Jewish living experience for the summer
months:
Camp Ramah provides the opportunity of a total
Jewish living experience for children and teenagers in the
relaxed atmosphere of a summer camp. Values built on a
shared experience and emotion reach our sons and
daughters in a manner impossible in a formal school
program. Over the years, RAMAH has helped instill a
deeper commitment to Judaism in many of its
participants.
The Center believes that benefits accrue to the congregation, not just to the individual. We have
therefore made monies available to reduce the financial cost of qualified students regardless of need.
These monies are not part of the regular Center budget, but rather are raised by donations to the Myra
Thier Lazarus Ramah Fund, and Sisterhood, and from other sources.
Two families have helped us greatly. The Gordon Family has established a scholarship fund in memory
of the beloved Robert J. Gordon. A long list of deserving boys and girls have been able to go-to Ramah
because of this scholarship. Abe Selber established in his will a perpetual trust for specific Jewish
education purposes. With the full support of his children, Iris and Franklin, who have never lost interest
in the Center, the proceeds of the Abe Selber Trust have been used to send campers to Ramah.
The following students attended these summer programs:
CAMP RAMAH
Kim Baruch, Reuben Bressler, Katherine Echt, Beth Greenburg, Laura Greenburg, David
Lazarus, Manya Lazarus, Pam Singal and Wendy Hirsch (Staff Member)
RAMAH ISRAEL SEMINAR PARTICIPANTS
Judith Abramson, Todd Berman, Sabrina Katz and Janet Jacobson (Staff Member)
U.S.Y. PILGRIMAGE
Katie Freedman, Sam Kersun, Irene Mann
SUMMER PROGRAM CO-ORDINATOR: HORTY GENDZIER

Torah Readers
“Klee Hemdah-A Precious Instrument”
The Torah is an endless source of delight, as indicated by the name of this report. It can never grow old. Every time we
study it new meanings are derived, new insights are acquired. It is no wonder that Judaism accords it its highest respect,
a synagogue is distinguished by its Ark for the Torah.
Each year new projects are planned for Torah Readers, to enlarge the number of participants and their adeptness. An
adult-study class was conducted by Larry Bernard. A group of adult readers was developed who showed their
accomplishments for the Sidra of Tzav on March 26. They included: Dr. Larry Goldberg, Norman Moss and Jack
Silverman.
The latest project is an attempt to devise a consistent pattern of study on a daily basis that will assure maximum
fluency and acquaintanceship. This pattern is named HAHAMAH, the numerical equivalent of all the verses in the
Torah. An outline form of every chapter and verse is given to each participant. He is asked to record the date each verse
is studied, one per day. A number of outstanding students have accepted this difficult task. One of these forms is posted
on the main hallway bulletin board. We hope that this new project will bring us closer to fulfillment of the goal of Isaiah,
Chapter 42: “To render the Torah great and glorious”.
Each year we are happy to add a new group of post-Bar Mitzvah Torah Readers. For 1982-83 they are: Simeon
Abramson, Richie Davis, David Goldfield, Michael Gordon, Ross Jeneff, Dennis Mizrahi, Ricky Plotkin,
Steven Shapiro.
Mickey Ehrlich
Vice President
Thomson McKinnon Securities, Inc.
Wishes You A Happy New Year

Amity II
Officers
President Izzy Weiss
Vice President Sonny Sandler
Treasurer Byron Morris
Recording Secretary Joan Marón
Corresponding Secretary Idlene Slaff
Board Members: Shirley & Milton Felder, Maida & Joe
Fraden, Beverly & Abbie Foster, Anita & Sid Garfinkel,
Bernice & Irvin Ghelerter, Helen & Joe Goldman, Muriel &
Seymore Lodinger, Bea & Marvin Sherman, Sam & Delores
Silberstein, Martha & Sully Savage, Sylvia Weiss, Marilyn
Sandler, Sonya Morris, Bernie Marón, and Norman Slaff.
The original Amity Club was organized in December, 1951, and for many years was a great opportunity for the young
Jewish couples in our community to become acquainted and to enjoy a sociable evening together once a month. It continued
for many years before disbanding.
Feeling the need for an Amity Club again, a group of couples headed by Sylvia and Izzy Weiss sent fliers out and an open
organizational meeting was held on February 17, 1983. At this meeting, it was agreed to call ourselves Amity II, to have three
events a year including New Year’s Eve, and that membership be limited to 135 couples.

Scoutmaster
Assistant Scoutmasters
Troop Committee Chairman
District Commissioner
BOY SCOUT TROOP 14
Randy Silverberg
,... Bill Wilensky
and Steve Davis
¡ Steve Ghelerter
Clara Mann
First Row: Doug Davis, Marty Gottlieb, Jeff Ghelerter,
Robbie Moore, Leon Jackler, Second Row: Steve Davis,
Bill Wilensky, Wesley Sheffield, Randall Silverberg,
Steve Ghelerter, Not Shown: Derek Blackshire, Jacob
Sail, Sean Usoff, Michael Weitzen.
Throughout the past year, the boys of Troop 14 have been busy “blazing trails.” In October, the Troop visited the University
of Florida campus for homecoming weekend. The Troop 14 contingent lodged at the Hillel Foundation where members
participated in Shabbat services. Several scouts accepted invitations to read the Torah portion from the Holy Scrolls. The
boys attended Gator Growl featuring comedian Robin Williams and an outstanding fireworks display. The next day Scouts
watched the Florida team romp its homecoming opponent. Exploration of the school campus included a trip to Fraternity
Row.
The Troop camped for the weekend in 28 degree weather at Camp Echockotee in December. The hardy scouts learned
many scouting skills including firestarting, axemanship, and knots not to mention the fine art of preparing beef stew.
Fernandina's Fort Clinch State Park provided the arena for another outdoor weekend. The Troop roughed it in the densely
wooded, secluded camping area near the roaring surf of the Atlantic. The campout included a tour of the Civil War era Fort
Clinch, a midnight snipe hunt and a fog shrouded cross-country hike to the longest fishing pier on the Florida East Coast.
Fort Wilderness at Disney World played host for a weekend to Troop 14 during which the boys enjoyed the less tranquil
frontier of the futuristic EPCOT Center.
Among the numerous other events the scouts enjoyed this past year are: a 5-mile hike to the Museum of Arts and Sciences
by way of the scenic River Road area punctuated by lunch at Worman's Deli; weekend camping at the nationally acclaimed
Scout World; and a benefit basketball game featuring stars from the Miami Dolphins. The weekly meeting agenda included
scouting skills, games, sports and public speakers of interest.
Troop 14 welcomed eight new ardent members this year, Leon Jackler, Doug Davis, Michael Weitzen, Marty Gotlieb,
Derrick Blackshire and Wes Sheffield. Four troop members including Patrol Leader Jeff Ghelerter, Sean Usoff, Doug Davis
and Leon Jackler experienced a week of intensive scouting and fun at Baden Powell Scout Reservation near Melrose for
summer camp.
Boy Scout Troop 14 can be truly proud of its activities and welcomes boys of 11 years and over to join its ranks.
Camp Site at Ole Timers' Campout
Channel 4 News Covers Campout
Troop 14's First Annual Ole Timers' Campout and Reunion celebrating its 64th Anniversary at Boy Scout Camp
Echockotee was an overwhelming success. Seventy guests and alumni “relived the best of the good ole days.” The event took
place June 3-5, 1983, and was augmented with commemorative T-shirts and reunion booklets with pictures tracing the history
of the Troop since its founding in 1919 by Rabbi S. Stein. The nostalgic weekend gained community-wide significance when the
event was featured on the nightly news by two local television stations.

Rabbi's Message
Our Prayers For Life
Many centuries ago, the Jew recognized the truth that “Man does not
live by bread alone” and that the good of life resides within, rather than
outside of man. Our prayers for life on these Yamim nora-im, the High
Holidays are a petition for more than the raw material of vitality or length
of days. Methuselah lived, the Bible tells us, for nine hundred thirty years,
and yet his presence is hardly noted other than for a few brief verses in
the text. Moses lived, relatively speaking, for a briefer period of time, and
yet his virtue and deeds are generously extolled. The issue of life is not in
its length, but in its content.
The Jew's attitude to life enabled him to outlive his oppressors and to
nullify all the instruments of persecution marshalled against him. The
history of Israel is a dramatization of the power of the human
determination and will over external circumstance.
The Jew survived because, paradoxically, survival was not his chief goal. He fought not merely against
disappearance and death, but for life; life allied to purpose. The words of the psalmist are significant.. .“I
shall not die, but live. . .to declare the works of the Lord”. Israel did not seek to make its history a
monument to endurance. It wished, rather, to turn it into an altar to God. The Jew survived because he
strove to live significantly.
How has the Jew defined significant living? What makes it meaningful and vital? The history of the Jew
is itself an ethical document which suggests the answers given over the centuries.
“To live means to learn.” Torah was indispensable to survival. Living in a world of shrines, altars,
temples, and courts, the Jew could state: “Torah is greater than priesthood or royalty”. Outer pressures,
the bitter struggle for existence, were not permitted to divert the Jew from study. One of the luminous
memories of my childhood, is the throng of merchants, peddlers, and small storekeepers who, at the end
of an exhausting day, assembled for study in the synagogue.
During eras of darkness, when a cultural blackout prevailed, the Biblical phrase could justly be applied
to the Jew, “But all the children of Israel had light in their dwellings”. Study was not an instrument of
survival or preparation of life.. .study was life. The Jew learned not as a luxury but as a necessity. And,
when Jerusalem was destroyed and the Jewish people lost its independence, Israel survived by building a
modest center of study at Yavneh. This quest for knowledge, this restless urge to learn is still with us as
an unspent force of the past. To live as a Jew meant to learn.
To live meant to feel. . .to respond with tenderness and compassion to our fellow men. The
Jew has understood that we come more fully to life as our outgoing sympathy links us with others. The
Dead Sea supports no life, for it has no outlet. Thus it is with man. A confined, self-enclosed spirit shrivels
and inevitably dies. The Jew, despite the harshness and cruelty meted out to him, never answered with
bitterness and vindictiveness. Instead, himself the victim of force, the Jew exalted gentleness and loved
mercy. The heroes whom he glorified were men of learning and kindness. Rabbi Akiva, the greatest of
post-biblical figures, found the central doctrine of Jewish teaching in the simple phrase, “Love thy
neighbor as thyself”. The marks of a Jew, our tradition teaches, are compassion, modesty, and kind
deeds. To Bve meant to feel and to care.
“To live meant to believe.”The Jew always had an inquiring and challenging mind and did not fear to
submit universally accepted and inherited ideas to analysis. One thinks of the prophets rebelling against
practices consecrated by usage, and of Job and Koheleth challenging the conventional views of their
(Continued on Page 19)

Cemetery Committee of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center
fcSi
Kenneth Bowden
The Cemetery Committee provides a very necessary function of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It is very important that the
Cemetery grounds are well kept and provide a peaceful resting place for our loved ones.
Our Cemetery Committee is chaired by Michael Schneider. Serving on the committee are Hymie Selber, Alan Taffet, Dr. Joe
Witten, Dr. Sam Witten, Jack Bettman, Joel Bressler, Nathan Sandler, Jack Becker and Joe Becker.
Mr. Kenneth A. Bowden, Controller of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, also serves as general manager of our Cemetery. It is
his responsibilty to oversee the upkeep and maintenance of the grounds. He has an excellent working relationship with Mr.
Jennewine who is general manager of Evergreen Cemetery. Mr. Jennewine has been very generous with his time and his staff,
Mr. Campbell, Mr. Miller and Mrs. Phillips. They have all taken excellent care of the Center's property, above and beyond what
is expected. We appreciate everything they do.
Johnnie B. Mercer, who has been custodian of the cemeteries for 24 years, is in virtual retirement. Maintenance is now
handled by Russell and Winifred Baker, á very dependable couple. We are very appreciative of the help given, us by John
Bergeron of the Southern Monument Company.

Girl Scout Troop #162
Leader: Clara Mann
Jewel Ann Achord, Sarah Andriesse, Judith Jacobson,
Christine Hester, Kimberly Kirkland, Melissa Myers, Sundi
Simpson, Jennie Schwab, Cheryl Stone.
For many years, the Center Sisterhood has actively sponsored the Brownie and Girl Scout Troops of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center. Scouting is a very important phase of the youth program in the Center. It helps develop character, usefulness at
home, appreciation of nature and the out-of-doors, creation of objects of beauty and usefulness from simple things, and it
helps these young people to find their places in the synagogue and community through service.
Under the able guidance of Mrs. Clara Mann, the entire Brownie and Girl Scout program provides all of these opportunities
for our young women of tomorrow. For further information please call Mrs. Mann.
Cub Scout Pack 14
Cub Scout Committee: Robin Robbins, Jeff
Flaschen, Lorraine Jenkins, Lanny Zimmerman,
Carole Goldberg, David Steinfeld, Debbie
Gottleib.
Cub Scouting is an introduction to Boy Scouts and plays an
outstanding role for boys between the ages of 8 and 10. The
boys advance from Wolf to Bear and then to Webelos. In this
day and age when there are so many temptations, our boys
find themselves interested in completing their achievements
toward a higher rank, and participating in the monthly pack
meeting. There, they put on skits and show off their crafts.
During the year, they share in many new and different
experiences - Annual Pinewood, Scout Sabbath, the Blue and
Gold Banquet, skating party, magic show, and a campout.
The Center is very indebted to Mr. Lanny Zimmerman for all
of his dedication to the Cub Scout program, and we know
that he will be needing your support for the coming year. If
you can be of some help, please contact the Recreation Office
at the Center.

Mount Carmel Gardens
Top row, left to right:
Abe Sugarman, Treasurer
Mildred Shafner Recording Secretary
Bottom Row, left to right:
Dorothy Levine 1st Vice President
Pauline Schemer .. Corresponding Secretary
Eugene Henry President
Eunice Safer 2nd Vice President
The improvements in our building during the past year have included, a new roof, which had been in the planning for some
time. The offices on our ground floor, vacated by the Jewish Federation, have been remodeled and renovated, and now,
besides the new offices for our Administrator and Co-Administrator, we have a clinic, a ceramic room, a card room, a library,
offices for the Nutrition Center, more space for our maintenance equipment, and of course a newly painted auditorium, with
ceiling fans, a beautiful mural wall, and a shiny floor. A place for everything, and everything in its place.
The most recent in safety devices is an inter com, installed on each floor, with push button connection to the desk, where
the individual can talk directly to the person on duty and tell them of any emergency that may have arisen. These, together
with the fire and smoke alarm, the daily door check, someone at the desk twenty-four hours a day, and the “panic” buttons in
each apartment, assures us of maximum protection at all times.
Another big incentive that draws new tenants to move here with us is, of course, the Kosher Nutrition Site, where all who
wish are served a salt-free, sugar-free lunch, five days a week. Meeting here on a regular basis is Mount Carmel Chapter of the
AARP #350; the Hannah Senesch group of Hadassah; Upjohns’ Clinic once a month; and podiatrist Steven Merritt twice a
month. Also, the Mount Carmel Newsletter is distributed free once a month.
We have Bingo, monthly birthday parties, frequent dances with live music, Christmas-Chanukah parties, an elaborate New
Years Eve party, and frequent visits to Theater Jacksonville, the Alhambra Dinner Theater, all activities sponsored by the
Mayor and others for Senior Citizens, and many ballets and music shows that come to Jacksonville.
The Mount Carmel Entertainers are recognized citywide for their outstanding showmanship, and have received many
awards for their volunteer performances. This group can be booked by calling Mary Foor, at 737-2238, and they will perform
for any club, organization or convalescent home.
Mount Carmel has become well known in our community as a 17-story high-rise, where Senior citizens live in comfort,
convenience and security. All 208 apartments of friendly people are involved in social, volunteer and educational projects, that
keep them active, with no time for boredom. We have a “bakers’ dozen” school aid volunteers, who have received television
and newspaper coverage - with our Frances Beerman being named Senior Citizen of the Year.
We are sponsored by the Jacksonville Jewish Federation, and are, all-in-all, the “In Place” for both non-Jews and Jewish
Senior Citizens.

Progressive
The Progressive Credit Union announced its assets
have increased to over a half million dollars. A dividend of
10% was declared in 1982 on share balances. This
represents a milestone in progress for the cooperative
movement organized in 1940 to provide financial services
to deserving persons of the Jewish faith.
The Credit Union offers many diversified services at
very low rates. Current automobile loans are at about 11%
annual percentage rate and signature loans are about 12%
annual percentage rate. This year the Credit Union
announced a new program to encourage small loans $300 -
$500 for individual needs at a small nominal charge.
Particulars are available from the Credit Union office.

Jewish War Veterans
Lt. Meyer Leibovitz Post No. 199
Commander Arnold Andriesse
Quartermaster Marty Caplan
Adjutant Marcus Grebler
Chaplain John Perin
The Hebrew Union Veterans (now Jewish War Veterans) was founded as a direct result of slander and insinuation that
Jews had not participatd in the military during the Civil War. By 1860, however, Jews had become an accepted part of the U.
S. Military.
Jewish War Veterans maintains a strong united front in Congress, making its voice heard on subjects such as foreign policy,
civil rights, defense spending, veterans benefits, housing and education.
Through development of national positions on these issues, J.W.V. helps to insure an effective democracy and the
protection of our rights as Jews, Americans, and veterans. Hospital programs, veterans and dependents rehabilitation,
scouting, summer camp scholarships, community activities - J.W.V. involvement means a better life for everyone.
Every year J.W.V. Post 199 participates in the Memorial Day ceremonies. This year, Memorial Day ceremonies took place
at 3 p.m. on Sunday, May 29th at Evergreen Cemetery. Colors of all veterans groups were presented and a wreath was laid.
As one local project this past year, we collected thousands of magazines and distributed them to the hospitals in this area,
with our name, Jewish War Veterans, on each magazine.
Membership in J.W.V. is open to all honorably discharged members of the armed forces of the U.S. including those who
have served for a period of 90 days or more.
For information, contact:
Marty Caplan
3114 Honeywood Dr., Jax. 32211
744-2607

===== PAGE 201 =====
Sisterhood Adopts Russian Family
Both design engineers, Michail Kalendarev was born in 1918 and his wife Evgenia Evseevna was born in 1923. They have two
children: Boris (B1957) and Mila Zisman who now lives in Israel.
The Kalendarevs first applied for visas in Nov. 1973. Their request was denied on grounds that Evgenia had access to
classified material. The family was told they would have to wait ten years for their permit. Evgenia was just a package designer
and had no knowledge of radio electronics. Michail was demoted and made to change his place of work. Both were out of
work for a long time.
Boris became the target of extreme harrassment. In 1976, he was expelled from the Poly Technical Institute in Leningrad
because his sister emigrated to Israel. They also banned him from the obligatory course in Military Training. Later Boris was
dismissed from the University of Leningrad and his flat was ransacked. In 1979, he was arrested for evading conscription. His
parents were refused permission to see their son until after the trial. Boris was sentenced to two years hard labor.
Please write:
USSR
RSFSR
Leningrad 196070
Basseinaya 12/81
Kalendarev, Michail

Dear Friends,
Women's American Ort is the Organization for Rehabilitation through Training. Ort is the vocational and technical
education of the Jewish people. For 102 years, since Ort's inception in Russia, we have been striving to give Man and Woman
a greater opportunity in life - given a trade and skill, an individual can maintain dignity and pride.
Woman's American Ort is the most complex and one of the fastest growing Jewish Women's organizations in the United
States. We are an activist group, not only taking stands, but positively working to upgrade the quality of life for ourselves, our
families, and our communities. We are women who are aware of the changes that are occuring within our society and are
concerned by the resurgence of global anti-semitism and other anti-democratic forces which threaten Jewish life, Israel's
survival, as well as pluralism, tolerance and freedom for all people, especially Soviet Jewry, and support for Israel and
American Jews.
Our involvement goes beyond career and vocational education. We are concerned about public education and strive
towards an up-grading of the level of education within the schools in the United States. We also have our first school (highly
acclaimed) in New York and a second, now being built in Los Angeles. Ort plays a major role in the new Jewish High School in
North Miami where Ort's expertise helps create the total curriculum, and runs the science and technological tracts.
Presently, we have 800 installations of various types of schools in 24 countries on 5 continents with two million student
graduates in our 102 year history. We are recognized as the finest vocational educators in the world, and the government
acknowledges our expertise by the presence of Ort members on educational governmental boards on the very echelon of
government.
Our chapters are as pluralistic as our country. Their diversity creates the right place for you, and you create the chapters.
Our 550 members in Jacksonville and the 140,000 in the United States, help give you strength to make your voice heard on all
issues that affect us today, and our children tomorrow.
Won't you make Women's American Ort stronger? Now, more than ever, we need the support of every woman who cares
about the present and the future. The children who we train today, are the working force of tomorrow.
247

WELCOME ABOARD THE
J.J.C. PRINCESS
Saturday, May 28,1983
Every one of the lucky people who attended this year's raffle enjoyed the vicarious experience of sailing on a cruise ship.
The food, fun and entertainment and special effects all added to the excitement of the evening.
Captains of our ship, Linda DuBow and Alan Gordon did a fabulous job of co-ordinating the ticket sales. The special effects
were created by chairman Isabel Balotin and Marilyn Garber with assistance from Shirley Bielski, Sandi Burman, Barbara
Safer, Randee Steinberg and Tim Riley. As usual the delicious food was catered by the Center Catering Service under the
direction of Blanche Slott.
Super Salesman, Nat Gold accepts
prize for Leonard Wolf.
Blanche Slott also bought a lucky ticket.
Linda DuBow carefully places winning
tickets on board.
A cheerful crowd of over 350 enjoyed
the evening's entertainment.
Perla Graff beams as she accepts her
winnings from Alan Gordon & Linda
DuBow.

Purim Play
All the majesty of the Purim story unfolded dramatically in the richly produced, superbly presented play, “Esther and the
King of Shushan.”
The production was an artistic blend of the fabulous music of the “King and I” and an original script collaborated by Isabel
Balotin, Shirley Bielski, and Marilyn Garber. The music lighting, sound system, and direction set a new standard of
professionalism in the Center’s reputation of high quality entertainment.
We are extremely proud of our very talented and versatile members who donated their time to make this a very outstanding
production.

KADIMA
Today Jewish families look to the synagogue as the center for Jewish activity: religious, education, social and recreational. A
major goal of the synagogue and its school is the education of youth with a firm base of knowledge, Jewish identification of youth,
a sense of pride in their heritage, and a commitment to Judaism. The goal of Kadima is to bring about the internalization of Jewish
values, attitudes and behavior patterns by supplementing the child’s formal religious education with informal Jewish learning
experience.

four synagogues of Jacksonville. It provides area Jewish youth

Florence Robin Retires
After 27 years of dedicated service to the Jacksonville Jewish Center, Florence Robin has
retired as Executive Secretary.
In her career, Florence has worked with Rabbis, Executive Directors and Center presidents,
adapting to the mannerisms and style of each without missing a step. Her work was always 100%
perfect.
Her pleasant manner, willingness to help and her excellent secretarial skills were an asset to the
Center. As a token of the congregation's appreciation, the Board of Directors honored Florence
at a Board Meeting on Wednesday evening, May 11. We wish Florence health and happiness.

Message from the
Administrative Director
As Administrative Director, I am involved in all phases of
Synagogue activities. , If ,i$ a privilege to serve a
congregation that continues to grow. I am always available
to assist an individual member or an organization with
plans and arrangements for the use of our very functional
facilities.
Our beautiful Setzer Hall and garden cannot be equalled
for its magnificence when used for dinners or receptions
for weddings, Bar/Bat Mitzvah, and organizational affairs.
The Fletcher Selber Auditorium serves as a lunch room for the Day School, a theater for stage productions and films, a
perfect area for dances and athletic events, and is open for men's Drop-In Basketball each Tuesday night.
We are proud that, through the generosity of our Board of Directors, we can offer Jewish service organizations the use of
our facilities for meetings at no charge, when scheduling permits.
The Center Catering staff has earned a reputation for excellence, and is qualified to serve your needs, from soup and
sandwich to a seven course dinner.
If you are a new member or a member of long standing, drop in. I will be happy to meet with you and acquaint you with our
dynamic programs and super facilities.
My husband, Louis, joins me in a sincere wish for a good year to the entire congregation.
Blanche Slott
Rabbi's Message (Continued from Page 15)
The skepticism of Israel, however, did not end with negation. The Jew believed that justice and right
were rooted in the nature of the universe and were not an excursion into fantasy. He believed that man
was clothed with cosmic dignity and as a child of God enjoyed irrevocable rights. Therefore, the Jew
never accepted the misfortune of the day as final, nor did he make the mood of the moment a permanent
philosophy. Because the Jew believed, he never descended as a people into the abyss of despair. He was
able to resist the temptations to defeatism and pessimism.
It was an upsurge of this classic Jewish faith that lead Jews in the Warsaw ghetto to sing in their
darkest hour, “Say not that this is the end of the road”. A familiar phrase was turned by these martyred
heroes into a triumphant hymn, as they sang unto death, “Ani maamin, ani maamin”, “I believe, I
believe”. There are questions that baffle the mind. There are sorrows that pierce the heart. There are
mysteries that challenge the most intelligent spirits. The Jew however believed, and in believing he
continued to live.
Perhaps, this should be the thrust of our prayers for the New Year. Our prayers for life will be
answered if we choose to make it so. Let us pray that we continue to learn, to feel and to care, and to
believe. . .so that we may live.
Inge and I, and the members of our family wish you and yours a year of health, happiness, purpose,
blessing, and peace. Amen.
Rabbi David Gaffney

Simhat Torah Honorees
Sunday, Oct. 10, 1983 — 12 Tishri 5742
DR. NATHAN SCHNEIDER,
Hatan Torah
Dr. Nathan Schneider is a con¬
cerned and dedicated Jew who
takes a sincere interest in the well¬
being of his family, friends and
synagogue.
This year he assumed the reins
of the Religious Committee with
confidence and ease after being a
member of that committee for
many years. Nathan was also
Chairman of the Education Com¬
mittee and an avid worker on the
Adult Education Committee and
Board of Directors, charter mem¬
ber of the Minyanaires and pres¬
ently serves as a Minyan Captain.
He has also taught Hebrew for the
Hebrew Literacy Campaign.
Nathan recently retired from the
Department of Health where he
was manager of the Public Health
and Medical Laboratory. This posi¬
tion brought him distinguished
recognition in his field.
The Center is proud to have a
man of Nathan’s calibre on the
Board of Trustees and as a leader
of our congregation. Sharing this
Simha with Nathan are his wife
Thelma and sons, Dr. Victor Sch¬
neider and Michael Schneider and
his six grandchildren.
LAWRENCE DuBOW
Hatan Bereshit
Laurie was chosen for his sus¬
tained involvement and contribu¬
tion of time, intelligence and energy
on behalf of the Center Building
Fund. Although his executive busi¬
ness responsibilities take him all
over the country, he still finds time
to spend many hours on Center
business. He served two years as
President of the Center. During
his tenure major changes were
made in our building and large
gifts were contributed.
As a speaker he commands atten¬
tion, not by virtue of his position but
by the essence of his personality.
He is one of the most eloquent and
forceful speakers of the Center and
has been most articulate in ex¬
plaining the Center’s plans and
prospects during his annual High
Holiday Synagogue Appeal. On
several social occasions, Laurie
served as Master of Ceremonies with
his usual charm and wit, mak¬
ing each affair an outstanding suc¬
cess.
In all his efforts on behalf of the
Center, he is always supported by
his wife Linda, a fabulous fund
raiser in her own right. His chil¬
dren, Susan and Michael, share in
the honor as do Laurie’s parents,
Bill and Anne DuBow.
CLARA MANN,
Woman of Valor
Our Woman of Valor, Clara
Mann, is a woman totally devoted
to the youth of our congregation
and community. Her dedication to
the Scouting program has brought
her recognition locally and nation¬
ally, as well.
She served as Sisterhood Hospi¬
tality Chairman, member of the
Youth Commission, volunteer Li¬
brarian for the Day School Library,
and volunteer teacher’s aid. Clara
is the President of Afternoon Ort
and Parliamentarian for Sisterhood.
She describes herself as “a person
willing to help whenever help is
needed.” Because of her loyal and
dedicated nature, Clara has stead¬
fastly contributed to the ongoing
Scouting program of the Jewish
Center.
Clara and her husband. Dr. Mil¬
ion Mann, are world travelers.
Wherever they have ventured, they
have made it a priority to investi¬
gate the Jewish community and
share their experiences with us.
She is a devoted mother to her
children, Irene and Ian, who is
presently studying at the Jewish
Theological Seminary. Because of
her positive influence, her children
have been very involved in the
youth program of the Center.
Daily Minyan
The Jacksonville Jewish Center takes pride in its Daily Minyan.
More than 100 different men are involved in this mitzvah. Through
the tireless efforts of the captains and co-captains, we have
minyans every morning and evening.
The anniversary date of the passing of a loved one presents an
opportunity to observe a mitzvah, if not a double mitzvah. The
mitzvah of observing the Yahrzeit is obvious-the second mitzvah
is the opportunity to insure a minyan so that others besides
yourself can also observe or attend a service for their own reasons.
The Daily Minyan needs you. For a satisfying and rewarding
experience attend once or twice a month on a regular basis.
Weekday evenings are in need of men to make a minyan, please
make an effort to attend whenever possible.
We attribute our success to the following minyan
captains:
SUNDAY .... Philip Selber, a.m.-Nathan Shmunes &
Bill Goldberg, p.m.

Horty Gendzier, p.m.

CALENDAR 217
Message From The Editor
As we marched on San Jose Blvd. on Yom Torah - You were there - carrying a Torah, a
banner, “a new heart and a new spirit”, to our new home. We stood in the bare Sanctuary
and we cheered, sang, danced, cried, - Together.
We were there, in our Synagogue Center, sharing simchas and sorrows - Together.
And side by side, we volunteered for fund raisers and socials, always working, and caring,
and striving to make the Jacksonville Jewish Center the “BEST”.
As you can see, the key word is “Together”, for there lies our strength and success. Our
Synagogue is too large for one person or small group to handle our varied programs. All of
us must work together, and strive for a stronger, more cohesive Synagogue Center.
I am grateful for the opportunity of working and volunteering at the Jacksonville Jewish
Center. I look forward to seeing many of you this coming year. My husband Allen, my
children and I wish you a New Year of happiness and peace.
7

“Our extraordinary Ark is a sculpture depicting the biblical story of Jacob's Ladder. . .it suggests that through prayer, the
Jew, as Jacob, rises heavenward. Through Torah, an expression of His love for His people, God descends to earth. The
Torah, the source of the teaching and guidance of Jewish history and tradition, is the means by which God makes His place
among us.” Rabbi David Gaffney
The following is a translation of the verses which appear on the Ark sculpture and Aron Kodesh.

Our beautiful Ark and surrounding, contemporary, majestic sculpture was designed by Israeli artist, AMI SHAMIR. Under
his capable direction, the ark was erected in time for the High Holidays, 5743.
The magnificence of the ark has added an awe-inspiring and elegant quality to our sanctuary. It is now indeed a perfect place
where we can dedicate ourselves to the ideals of Judaism. . .a place where we can sanctify life's significant occasions and
above all, a beautiful setting in which to pray.
A special thank you to members of the Interior Design Committee, especially its chairman, Philip Selber, whose tenacity
and hard work assured its completion.
CREDITS: Sketch of Ark—Blanche Slott; Translation and sources of verses—Rabbi David Gaffney, Ted Gross and Joel
Bressler.

Consecration Classes
The first grade children and new students of the Solomon Schechter Day School along with the Alef Class of the
Afternoon Hebrew School were consecrated in a ceremony at the Jacksonville Jewish Center on Saturday morning, April
30, 1983. The ceremony marks the beginning of the children’s formal Hebrew and Judaic education and is a very meaningful
event. The children receive certificates, miniature Torahs and the traditional jar of honey. The teacher for both classes was
Zipora Zucker.

Sunday School
The Sunday School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is planned expressly for children in Kindergarten, First and Second
grades. This two hour program provides each child the opportunity to experience the richness of their Jewish heritage
through song, dance, role playing, cooking, short stories, games, arts and crafts. Emphasis is placed on the holidays and
festivals.
This past year the program coordinator was Tricia Bloom. This year, Mrs. Peggy Organes will co-ordinate the program and
will be assisted by Betsy Gay, Alina Kentof and Robin Rand.

Center Catering Service
Our catering service is available for Bar/Bat Mitzvahs,
Weddings, Anniversaries, and other festive occasions. We
offer a variety of meat, fish, and dairy menus, and can
provide salad bars, buffets, or plated service. Prices vary
according to the menu and attendance.
The Center is now offering “Prestige Kosher Catering”
under the personal supervision of Rabbi Gaffney.
Planning and Kitchen direction are supervised by our
Administrator, Blanche Slott, assisted by Sandy Shapiro -
head cook - Annie Mae Fort.

===== PAGE 247 =====
Adult Study 5743
“OUR GOAL IS TO PUT JUDAISM INTO THE LIVES OF OUR
MEMBERS, AND LIFE INTO THEIR JUDAISM”
Every culture has its passion, its fixation, its reason for being. Every
civilization has some element that evokes its zeal, its ultimate loyalty, its
greatest sacrifice.
We Jews have such a passion, it is called Talmud Torah-the study of
Torah...Jewish education. Ingrained, almost hereditary, it was the focal
point of concern of the youngest child in the Heder to the eldest
patriarch studying Bible and Talmud. The Jewish community
encouraged adults to study. Education was not only for children nor was
it merely an instrument for pursuing a career. Education was the means
by which character was fashioned, the character of the individual, of the
Jew and the community.
Members of the Jacksonville Jewish Center and other members of the
Jewish community participated in a comprehensive Adult Education
program. Some of the courses offered were: Beginners and Intermediate
Hebrew, Haftorah and Torah Skills, Sisterhood Bar and Bat Mitzvah 10,
Bible Study with Commentary, Synagogue Skills, History of the Jewish
Experience, Israeli Folk Dancing.
THE
ff ADULT
^w8/// STUDY
INSTITUTE
Innovative Adult Study
THE JOY OF SIMHAS-A series of three in-depth
analysises of Jewish festivals was held. The classes and
workshops featured discussions on the history and
observance of Hanukkah, Purim, and Passover.
Knowledgeable resource people were invited to lead each
series.
The charming and witty Dr. Theodore Gaster, well-known author
and one of the translators of the Dead Sea Scrolls, discusses
“Purim - the Gansa Megillah”
ONCE IN A NEW MOON-This new adult study format
was designed for those interested students who work in
the downtown area. Each session took place once a month
(usually on a Wednesday closest to Rosh Hodesh) at
Baptist Hospital.
Rabbi David Gaffney led this very popular lunch and
learn program in which a variety of Jewish topics were
discussed.
SCHOLAR-IN-RESIDENCE-A total study weekend was
held on December 3-5 with the guest scholar-in-residence,
Rabbi Samuel Dresner. The weekend included Shabbat
Dinner, Service, Kiddush and a comprehensive weekend
of lecture and study with the well-known author.
Sisterhood Bar and Bat Mitzvah Class

The Jacksonville District of the Zionist Organization of
America has had a successful year. We have for over thirty
years, held our annual Jewish National Fund function
planting a grove of 1000 trees in Israel. This year's grove was
planted in memory of Sam Galinsky and in honor of Esther
Galinsky. In addition we co-sponsored a program with Beth
Shalom Congregation, Father Paul Ischi, “Lebanon One Year
Later.”
ZOA is our national organization which has been very
effective and important in our government for mobilizing
support of Israel.

Our constant gratitude, not only for making the publication possible,
but also for the support they lend to our Youth Activities. We urge
our members to reciprocate with their patronage.
• A reference and guide for the Jacksonville Jewish Center
membership and the Jewish community.
• Serves over 7,000 members and advertisers.
• All proceeds devoted to the Center's program.
• The only Jewish publication in the community listing all groups.
The Year Book is a summary of the various activities and programs of
this Synagogue-Center depicted in pictures and words. We are
grateful to the many organizations of this community and our
congregants who have cooperated in providing us with material used.
We appreciate the efforts of our staff and other members of the
Center who have devoted time and effort on behalf of our 1983-84
Center Yearbook.

===== PAGE 260 =====
Center Choral Society Concert
“Serve the Lord with gladness, come before His presence with singing.”
(Psalms 100:2)
The Annual Center Choral Society Concert was a
beautiful musical experience. Cantor Jeffrey Nadel
returned as the guest vocal artist and thrilled the
audience with his enchanting repertoire of classical &
Hebrew selections.
The soloists were delightful as well: Raymond
Cohen, Roz Klein, Paul Goldstein, Mary Haimowitz,
Debra Massave, Peggy Organes, Beth Shorstein. Judy
Geller served as assistant conductor and William
Saunders was the piano accompanist.
The Center takes great pride in its “home grown”
talents and the talents of those who recently joined the
Center family.
Our sincere thanks to Cantor Emeritus Abraham
Marton who co-ordinated this delightful event.
Cantor Jeffrey Nadel thrills the audience with his enchanting
voice.
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
takes pride in presenting
THE
Center Choral Society
Abraham Marton, Hazan Emeritus, Director of Music
Concert of Jewish Music
Guest Artist:
Tenor Jeffrey Nadel
Sunday, March 13, 1983, 7:30 p.m.
William Saunders, Accompanist
Judy Geller, Assistant Conductor
Cantor Marton peruses music with pianist,
William Saunders.
U-Thful Seniors (U.S.)
“Enthusiasm is the electricity of life”
A new and vital outreach program for mature adults
of the Center was initiated this year. Social, recreational
as well as religious and cultural programs were
scheduled and planned to suit the needs of the mature
adult community.
“Bob and Annette”, a musical duo, “kicked off” the
year with their unusual entertainment. A crowd of over
100 attended. Another favorite was a book review by
the humorous and eloquent Mary Wolf.
Picnics, special holiday celebrations and social
gatherings have been planned for the coming year. Be
there.

WHV ONE OF THE BIGGEST BANKS IN THE COUNTY STU
THNISASMAU. TOWN IS THE BEST PUCETO GROW UR
When William Barnett first opened his doors in 1877, he was determined to make his
institution into something more than a bank He knew then, as we do now, that a bank must
be intimately involved in the day-to-day concerns of the community it serves.
That’s why, despite our size and statewide reach, we’ll always do business
with a certain hometown feeling. It’s something we learned while growing up
in small towns across the state, and something we’ll never forget in its big cities.
Barnett BankofJacksonville

===== PAGE 268 =====
Marilyn Sandler
Administrator
Day Care & Pre-School
Programs
Willard Kennedy
Headmaster
Solomon Schechter
Day School
Tradition with Innovation
The Solomon Schechter Day School has continued to provide excellence in both General Studies and
Judaic Studies for the past 23 years. While we continue to offer traditional courses of study which are of
proven value, we also continue to grow by including new techniques and innovations which will enrich our
students experience. Computers are significant as an accepted part of our teaching and learning modes.
Junior Congregation, Torah reading and Trop are an integral part of our curriculum. Our students
enrich their lives by attending services and learning how to lead the service.
A course in sex education is taught by professionals. Enrichment programs are offered throughout the
year in the Pre-School/Kindergarten classes as well as upper grades.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center has provided an excellent Extended Day Care Program for the last two
years. Due to the success of this program, we have extended it. Day Care is available from 7:30 a.m. until
5:30 p.m. This is a Total Learning Program and an asset to the Center as well as the entire Jewish
community.
Laura Edwards Shirley Bielski

===== PAGE 275 =====
Havurot
The Havurah Movement was born out of the need of the American Jew to express his Jewish identity in a more personal
fashion than the institution of the modern synagogue could provide. Roughly translated, Havurah means fellowship, but, in its
truest sense, it means much more. It is a group of people with a common interest, who are committed to sharing and
expanding that interest together in a very personal way. The comaraderie and deep personal feeling which develops between
members can be one of life's most rewarding experiences.
Ananchnu-Mispacha
Havurah Ha-Rishionim
During the fall of 1983, an exciting Jewish Happening
occurred. Our Havurah had an organizational meeting
and our “Group of Friends” was launched. Since then,
we have met every month and have discussed topics
ranging from “An Endocrinologists View of the Bible”,
to Jewish Art, to the Holocaust. In addition to learning
together, we “schmooze” together and of course, we
always eat at least a little nosh together.
All of us are feeling super about the social,
intellectual, and spiritual stimulation our Havurah
provides. We are looking forward to continued personal
and group growth as a result of it.
The members of the Havurah are Ellen and Dr. Neil
Abramson, Marlene and Robert Bossen, Linda and
Joel Bressler, Ellen and Norman Freedman, Dr. Joseph
and Wendy Honigman, liana and Jack Manasse,
Sandy Shapiro, Ina and Alan Taffet, Dot and Harry
Verstandig.
This is a group consisting of ten couples who have
been meeting once a month over the past nine years.
These get-togethers that have been held in the homes of
those participating have proven to be a combination of
comaraderie and stimulating discussions. Topics such
as the Israeli conflict with her mid-east neighbors and
other pertinent Jewish problems are aired. On occasion
a passage in the Talmud is analyzed under the capable
guidance of Ted Gross.
Sidney & Frances Berkowitz, Leon & Shirley Schinasi,
Nathan & Ann Freidlin (not pictured), Bill & Grace
Goldberg, Ted & Eunice Gross, Dr. & Mrs. Nathan
Schneider, Nathan & Anne Shmunes, Jack & Mollie
Silverman, Ben & Eve Waldman, Samuel & Helen
Leibowitz.
Young Family Havurah
We first gathered three years ago, at Rabbi Gaffney's suggestion, to learn about Havurot. Those of us who were intrigued by
the idea made a tentative commitment to meet again. To be sure we were a group of young couples with young or expectant
families, but we were also a very diverse group personally, professionally, and in the depth of our religious commitment. We
have celebrated Hanukkah, built sukkot and enjoyed the warmth of a Shabbat weekend together. We have discussed current
events, anti-Semitism, feminism and Pirke Avot — Ethics of the Fathers — together. Best of all we have shared simchas: baby
naming, brith milah, a newly koshered kitchen, together. Our Havurah experience has added depth and texture to our lives.
Dr. Bruce and Cathy Yergin, Dr. Larry and Phyllis Goldberg, Eliot and Barbara Safer, Jack and Donna Bettman, Harry and Joni
Shmunes, Dr. Marco and Robin Rand, Larry and Carla Bernard, Jeffrey and Robin Morris, Bob and Etta Drashin, Dr. Alan and Jo Ellen
Marks.
